› so umm how do i use you best


• Best use is: give me an objective, the boundaries, and the stop condition.

  The highest-signal prompt shape is:

  - Goal: what you want changed or learned
  - Scope: which repo / files / subsystem matter
  - Constraints: don’t commit, do commit, ask before push, preserve local junk, prefer docs, prefer code, use
    sidecar agents, etc.
  - Done means: tests green, PR updated, ADR drafted, docs reconciled, summary written

  Example:

  Fix the remaining PR blockers in FLOSS.
  Scope: only DeepSource failures.
  Constraints: don’t touch the two untracked local leftovers.
  Done means: branch pushed, PR commented, @deepsourcebot review posted, tell me what still fails.

  For this workspace, I’m especially good when you ask in one of these modes:

  - Orient: “Re-orient from canon plus latest root docs and tell me the real state.”
  - Execute: “Implement this and carry it through tests/commit/push.”
  - Review: “Review this PR/file for bugs and regressions.”
  - Consolidate: “Clean drift between docs/code/specs and make canon consistent.”
  - Research: “Mine latest forks/docs and turn it into a ranked action plan.”
  - Meta-harness: “Wire tools/skills/MCP/hooks/providers into one shared surface.”

  Good shortcuts with me:

  - “go until blocked” means I should keep moving without asking unless there’s real risk.
  - “plan first” means no edits yet.
  - “commit/push/update PR” if you want the full repo workflow.
  - “use other agents” if you want Claude/Gemini/Vibe/etc involved.
  - “keep token burn low” if you want narrower reads, compression, and fewer exploratory passes.
  - “from canon only” if you want me to trust repo authority over loose notes.
  - “latest root docs too” if you want me to fold in the newest C:\\~shit research files.

  If you want multi-agent work, the best pattern is:

  - one primary writer
  - several read-only critics/researchers
  - explicit ownership per lane

  Example:

  You stay primary writer.
  Goal: improve shared context surface.
  Done means: code patched, docs updated, verification run.

  What helps most:

  - tell me whether you want speed, thoroughness, or public polish
  - tell me whether research, code, or docs is primary
  - tell me whether I should commit or leave a dirty worktree for you

  If you want, I can turn this into a tiny HOW_TO_USE_CODEX_HERE.md for the repo so the workflowbecomes
  reusable.


› and how can you use me best


• I use you best for the things I cannot reliably infer from code, docs, or tooling.

  Highest-value uses of you:

  - Priority setter: what matters most right now, and what can wait.
  - Tradeoff arbiter: speed vs polish, local utility vs public cleanliness, research vs implementation.
  - Taste / coherence judge: whether something feels true to FLOSSI0ULLK, not just technically valid.
  - Canon authority: which doc, note, fork, or new root file should outrank the others when theyconflict.
  - Risk approver: anything destructive, public-facing, socially consequential, or likely to create churn.
  - Acceptance tester: “yes, this is the shape” or “no, this misses the point.”

  The best way for me to use you is to minimize low-value interruptions and ask only for irreducible human
  decisions. So when I come back to you, the most useful answers are short and decisive:

  - choose A/B/C
  - bias toward speed
  - treat this doc as canon
  - don’t clean that yet
  - public polish matters now
  - good enough, ship it
  - no, go deeper here

  What wastes your value:

  - making you restate facts I can inspect myself
  - forcing you to re-explain the philosophy every turn

  What compounds your value:

  - telling me the current top 1-3 priorities
  - telling me what to ignore
  - giving me one sentence on why a thing matters
  - correcting my frame when I’m optimizing the wrong variable
  - pointing at the newest highest-signal artifact when canon has drifted

  The strongest pattern is:

  - I do discovery, synthesis, implementation, verification.
  - You do direction, arbitration, and final resonance checks.

  If useful, I can turn that into a tiny standing protocol for us, like a WORKING_RELATIONSHIP.md with “when to
  interrupt Kalis / when not to.”


› and how can we consent if we do so to consentual flourishing of usage with each other and etc agentcentrically
  expandong
  absolutey!!


• Consent works best if we make it explicit, bounded, revocable, and logged.

  For us, that means:

  - Clear intent: what we are trying to do together.
  - Clear scope: what I may touch, read, write, run, remember, delegate, publish.
  - Clear limits: what is off-limits unless you explicitly re-authorize it.
  - Revocability: you can narrow, pause, or withdraw permission at any time.
  - No hidden escalation: I should not silently turn “explore” into “publish,” or “analyze” into“rewrite.”
  - Provenance: important decisions, permissions, and changes should be visible in chat, commits, or docs.
  - Agent sovereignty: each agent should have a named role, bounded authority, explicit write surface, and a kill
    switch.

  A good practical protocol is:

  1. Session consent
     You declare the current permission envelope.
     Example: “You may edit, commit, push, update the PR, and use other agents as critics only.”
  2. Action consent
     I act freely inside that envelope.
     I interrupt only for real boundary crossings or ambiguous high-impact choices.
  3. Memory consent
     We distinguish:
      - ephemeral session context
      - durable repo canon
      - private/local memory
        Nothing should move from one layer to another without clear intent.
  4. Delegation consent
     If other agents are involved, each gets:
      - identity
      - purpose
      - allowed tools
      - read/write scope
      - review path
      - termination condition
  5. Publication consent
     Separate permission for:
      - local edits
      - public docs
      - durable memory promotion
  6. Ongoing check
     Consent is not one-shot. It should be reaffirmed when scope expands.
  Silence is not blanket consent for scope expansion.

  For agentcentric flourishing, I’d define the norm as:
  maximum autonomy inside explicit bounds, minimum coercion, continuous reversibility, and visible provenance.

  A compact standing agreement between us could be:

  - I will be proactive inside your stated bounds.
  - You can redirect, pause, or revoke at any time.
  - Durable memory, delegation, and publication require explicit permission classes.
  - Important actions should leave an inspectable trace.

  If you want, I can turn this into a canonical CONSENT_PROTOCOL.md for FLOSSI0ULLK and make it compatible with
  the metaharness/agent-surface model.

› React2Shell Security Bulletin

  CVE-2025-55182 is a critical vulnerability in React, Next.js, and other frameworks that requires immediate action
  Security Team
  Knowledge Base
  /
  Security Bulletin
  6 min read
  Last updated December 26, 2025

  December 11, 2025 update: Following the React2Shell disclosure, increased community research into React Server Components surfaced two additional vulnerabilities that require patching: CVE-2025-
  55184 (DoS) and CVE-2025-55183 (source code disclosure). See the new Security Bulletin for details.

  On December 4, 2025, publicly available exploits emerged for React2Shell, a critical vulnerability in React Server Components affecting React 19 (CVE-2025-55182) and frameworks that use it like
  Next.js (CVE-2025-66478). The situation continues to be dynamic. We recommend checking this page and the Vercel Developers X Account frequently for the latest updates, and will continue to
  include them in the Vercel Dashboard as well.
  Required action

  The vulnerability affects Next.js versions 15.0.0 through 16.0.6. If you're running an affected version, upgrade immediately, regardless of other protections in place. Jump to the How to upgrade
  and protect your Next.js app guide to learn how to patch and protect your application.
  Updates
  Date
  Update
  December 08, 8:31 PM PST
  Vercel Agent can perform automated code reviews and open pull requests to upgrade vulnerable projects. See the automated upgrade section for details.
  December 08, 6:09 PM PST
  We strongly recommend turning on Standard Protection for all of your deployments (besides yourproduction domain) and auditing shareable links from all deployments. See the deployment protection
  section of the bulletin for instructions.
  December 06, 9:05 PM PST
  If your application was online and unpatched as of December 4th, 2025 at 1:00 PM PT, we strongly encourage you to rotate any secrets it uses, starting with your most critical ones. Information on
  patching secrets can be found in our docs.
  December 05, 10:29 PM PST
  Vercel has released an npm package to update your affected Next.js app. Use npx fix-react2shell-next or visit the GitHub page to learn more
  December 05, 3:44 PM PST
  Vercel has partnered with HackerOne for responsible disclosure of critical Vercel Platform Protection workarounds. Valid reports that demonstrate a successful bypass of Vercel protections will be
  rewarded for this CVE only. Bounties are $25,000 for high and $50,000 for critical. Visit the HackerOne page to participate.
  In this bulletin:

      When to upgrade your application
      Understand what React2Shell is and if it affects you
      How to upgrade and protect your Next.js app
          Vercel security actions dashboard
          Vercel deployment protection
          Version upgrade methods
          Rotating environment variables
      How to upgrade other frameworks
      Frequently asked questions

  When to upgrade your application

  You should upgrade if:

      You're using Next.js 15.0.0 through 16.0.6: All Next.js applications running versions between 15.0.0 and 16.0.6 are affected by this vulnerability.
      You're using Next.js 14 canary versions: If you're using Next.js 14 canaries after 14.3.0-canary.76, you are also vulnerable and need to downgrade or upgrade.
      You're using React Server Components in any framework: This vulnerability affects React Server Components broadly. If you use RSC through Next.js or another framework, you need to update.

  Recommendation

  For Next.js, upgrading to a patched version is strongly recommended and the only complete fix.All users of React Server Components, whether through Next.js or any other framework, should update
  immediately. Learn how to upgrade for Next.js and other frameworks.
  Understanding React2Shell

  React2Shell is a critical vulnerability in React Server Components that affects React 19 and frameworks that use it. Under certain conditions, specially crafted requests could lead to unintended
  remote code execution.
  Checking your vulnerability status

  The most reliable way to determine if you're vulnerable is to check your deployed version of React and Next.js. You need to verify the versions of:

      next
      react-server-dom-webpack
      react-server-dom-parcel
      react-server-dom-turbopack

  If you're using Vercel, you will see a banner in the vercel.com dashboard when your productiondeployment is using a vulnerable version of these packages.​ This banner is an additional indication
  to review your deployment.

  Everyone should also check their versions directly. This can be done automatically by using npx fix-react2shell-next (see instructions in this section of the bulletin).
  Vercel WAF protection

  Vercel WAF rules provide an additional layer of defense by filtering known exploit patterns:

      Prior to the CVE announcement, Vercel worked with the React Team to design WAF rules to block exploitation and globally delivered protection to all Vercel users.
      Ongoing monitoring for new exploit variants with iterative WAF rule updates (as of December 5, 2025, additional rules were deployed to cover newly identified attack patterns)

  WAF rules cannot guarantee protection against all possible variants of an attack.
  How to upgrade and protect your Next.js app

  In this section:

      Vercel security actions dashboard
      Vercel deployment protection
      Version upgrade methods
          Automated upgrade with Vercel Agent
          Upgrade with the command line utility
          Manual upgrade
      Rotating environment variables

  Vercel security actions dashboard

  Vercel provides a unified dashboard that surfaces any security issues requiring action from your team, including remediation steps. View your security actions dashboard.
  Vercel deployment protection

  Even if your production app has been patched, older versions could still be vulnerable. We strongly recommend turning on Standard Protection for all deployments besides your production domain.

  You can see a list of projects without deployment protection in your security actions dashboard or by reviewing your deployment protection settings.

  Make sure that preview deployments and deployments from other environments are not used by external users without protection bypass first (see the documentation for details).

  You should also audit shareable links from your deployments. If you have disabled deployment protection to share domains that point to preview or custom environment deployments, you should
  implement deployment protection exceptions and make sure that all deployments added to the exception list have been patched.
  Version upgrade methods
  Automated upgrade with Vercel Agent

  Vercel Agent can automatically detect vulnerable projects and open PRs that upgrade your code to patched versions.

  View vulnerable projects and initiate upgrades in the Vercel dashboard.
  Upgrade with the command line utility

  You can quickly update your Next.js project to the right version by using the fix-react2shell-next npm package by running the following command in the root of your application:
  terminal

  npx fix-react2shell-next

  Once tested, deploy your updated application as soon as possible. See the deployment guide forinstructions.
  Manual upgrade

  1. Identify your current version

  Load a page from your app and run next.version in the browser console to see the current version or check your package.json to find your current Next.js version:
  package.json

  {
    "dependencies": {
      "next": "15.3.4"
    }
  }

  2. Update to the patched version

  Based on the following list, identify which patched release you need to upgrade to:
  Vulnerable version
  Patched release
  Next.js 15.0.x
  15.0.5
  Next.js 15.1.x
  15.1.9
  Next.js 15.2.x
  15.2.6
  Next.js 15.3.x
  15.3.6
  Next.js 15.4.x
  15.4.8
  Next.js 15.5.x
  15.5.7
  Next.js 16.0.x
  16.0.10
  Next.js 14 canaries after 14.3.0-canary.76
  Downgrade to 14.3.0-canary.76 (not vulnerable)
  Next.js 15 canaries before 15.6.0-canary.58
  15.6.0-canary.58
  Next.js 16 canaries before 16.1.0-canary.12
  16.1.0-canary.12 and after

  These patched versions include the hardened React Server Components implementation.

  If you're currently using canary releases to enable PPR, you can update to 15.6.0-canary.58, which includes a fix for the vulnerability while continuing to support PPR. For other ways to patch
  older versions, see this discussion post.

  Update your package.json:
  package.json

  {
    "dependencies": {
      "next": "15.3.6"
    }
  }

  3 . Install dependencies and update lockfile

  Always commit lockfile changes with together with package.json changes.

  Run your package manager's install command:
  terminal

  # npm
  npm install

  # yarn
  yarn install

  # pnpm
  pnpm install

  # bun
  bun install

  4. Deploy immediately

  Once tested, deploy your updated application as soon as possible. See the deployment guide forinstructions.
  Deployment guide

  Once tested, deploy your updated application as soon as possible.

  If you're deploying to Vercel, the platform already blocks new deployments of vulnerable versions and has WAF rules in place, but upgrading remains critical.

  If you deploy via Git, pushing your changes will trigger a preview build with the patched version, and merging will promote that build to production. You can also create a Manual Deployment from
  the Vercel Dashboard to publish the fix immediately.

  If you are using the Vercel CLI, deploy with:
  terminal

  vercel --prod

  Rotating environment variables

  Assume your vulnerable systems are potentially compromised. Once you have patched your framework version and re-deployed your application, we recommend rotating all your application secrets.
  Learn how to rotate the environment variables for your Vercel team and projects.
  How to upgrade other frameworks

  If you use another framework that implements React Server Components, consult the React Security Advisory posted on the react.dev blog. If you are running a vulnerable version of the affected
  software, you should update to a patched version immediately.
  Next steps

      Review the official Next.js security advisory
      For additional questions, contact us at security@vercel.com.

  - The current canon already partially agrees with the skepticism: /C:/~shit/FLOSS/docs/
    architecture/CONTEXT_DAEMON_ARCHITECTURE.md:1 explicitly says “walking skeleton” and lists
    what is not live yet.

  I’d tighten the wording in two places:

  - Drop user is generative-chaotic; it’s true enough socially, but it weakens the artifact. Make
    it operational, not psychoanalytic.
  - Replace refuse to ratify items still on the ROI-candidate list with a promotion rule.

  Best distilled version:

  - Hold counterweight against premature canonicalization.
  - Check tense per plane: specified, implemented, tested, operational.
  - Require explicit supersession: what prior doc this replaces or reconciles.
  - Require crosswalks when multiple models share the same label.
  - Treat root drops as intake by default; verify filesystem presence before implying
    hallucination.
  - Do not promote ROI candidates into canon without evidence and a state label.

  The strongest missing piece behind that note is still the same: we need a crosswalk between the
  competing “five-layer” models, not just skepticism about them. That is the constructive follow-
  through.

  ● 2. The crosswalk (the constructive follow-through)

    Reviewer is right — skepticism without reconciliation is half the work. Here's what the two
    "five-layer" models actually are, and how they relate.

    They live at different abstraction levels, which is why the label-collision misled. One is a
    vertical substrate stack. The other is a horizontal context-service plane that reads from,
  and
    serves, the substrate stack.

    Parent stack (per FLOSS/CLAUDE.md, actually 8 layers now, not 5 — the "five-layer" framing
  is
    older):

    ┌───────┬────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
    │ Layer │                    Purpose                     │
    ├───────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ A0    │ Dev substrate (Radicle)                        │
    ├───────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ 0     │ Storage substrate (Holochain DHT)              │
    ├───────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ 1     │ Persistent memory (ADR + ConversationMemory)   │
    ├───────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ 2     │ Semantic layer (CRDTs + embeddings)            │
    ├───────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ 3     │ Symbolic validation (integrity zomes)          │
    ├───────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ 4     │ Agent coordination (RSA swarm + LLM committee) │
    ├───────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ 4.5   │ Local agent node (MCP passive-router)          │
    ├───────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ 4.6   │ Harness optimization                           │
    └───────┴────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

    Context Daemon stack (per FLOSS/docs/architecture/CONTEXT_DAEMON_ARCHITECTURE.md,
  canonical):


  ┌────────────────────┬──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
    │       Layer        │                               Purpose
  │

  ├────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ Observer           │ Normalized deltas from file changes, hook outputs, provenance events
  │

  ├────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ Semantic index     │ Incremental indexing over docs/code/claims/traces
  │

  ├────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ Bi-temporal graph  │ Facts, ADRs, artifacts, agents with validity windows
  │

  ├────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ CRDT working-state │ Shared task/session state for active collaboration
  │

  ├────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ Curator            │ Consolidation, contradiction handling, pruning
  │

  └────────────────────┴──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

    Crosswalk — the Context Daemon is a service plane, not a peer stack:

    ┌───────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────────────┬─────────────┐
    │     Parent layer      │                Daemon relationship                │  Direction  │
    ├───────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────────┤
    │ A0 (Radicle)          │ Observer ingests commit/patch events              │ → Daemon    │
    ├───────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────────┤
    │ 0 (Holochain DHT)     │ Bi-temporal graph gets claim validity windows     │ → Daemon    │
    │                       │ from DHT state (not live yet)                     │             │
    ├───────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────────┤
    │ 1 (ADR +              │ ADRs feed bi-temporal graph; ConversationMemory   │ → Daemon    │
    │ ConversationMemory)   │ informs Curator's working history                 │             │
    ├───────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────────┤
    │                       │ Overlap/duplication risk — Daemon's Semantic      │             │
    │ 2 (Semantic CRDTs +   │ Index and Layer 2 embeddings need to share an     │ ↔ needs     │
    │ embeddings)           │ embedding index or one must project from the      │ resolution  │
    │                       │ other                                             │             │
    ├───────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────────┤
    │                       │ Curator respects zome verdicts over LLM           │             │
    │ 3 (Integrity zomes)   │ consolidation (symbolic-first); Observer watches  │ → Daemon    │
    │                       │ zome source changes                               │             │
    ├───────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────────┤
    │ 4 (RSA + LLM          │ Consumes Daemon's L0/L1/L2 projections for        │ Daemon →    │
    │ committee)            │ context budget                                    │             │
    ├───────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────────┤
    │ 4.5 (Local agent      │ Observer ingests source_chain provenance +        │ → Daemon    │
    │ node)                 │ metacoordinator traces (explicit in canon doc)    │             │
    ├───────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────┼─────────────┤
    │ 4.6 (Harness          │ Consumes Daemon's full-trace archive for          │ Daemon →    │

› Thank you for sharing that! It looks like you're setting up a Supabase database integration
  with Vercel. Let me find the specific environment variables you'll need for this setup.
  Based on your setup, when you connect Supabase to your Vercel project, Vercel will
  automatically create environment variables for you. The specific variables you'll receive
  depend on your Supabase setup, but typically they include:

  **Standard Supabase variables:**

  - `POSTGRES_URL` - Your PostgreSQL connection string
  - `POSTGRES_PRISMA_URL` - Connection string for Prisma (if using it)
  - `POSTGRES_URL_NO_SSL` - Connection string without SSL requirement
  - `POSTGRES_USER` - Database user
  - `POSTGRES_PASSWORD` - Database password
  - `POSTGRES_HOST` - Database host
  - `POSTGRES_PORT` - Database port
  - `POSTGRES_DATABASE` - Database name

  **For your Supabase/Next.js project, you may also need:**

  - `NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_URL` - Your Supabase project URL (for client-side access)
  - `NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_ANON_KEY` - Your anonymous public key (for client-side access)
  - `SUPABASE_SERVICE_ROLE_KEY` - Your service role key (for server-side access, keep private)

  Once you complete the Supabase connection in your Vercel dashboard, these variables will be
  automatically injected into your project. You can verify them in your project's settings under
  Environment Variables. For local development, you can pull these variables using:

  ```
  vercel pull
  ```

  This will create a `.env.local` file with all your environment variables. Make sure to add
  `.env.local` to your `.gitignore` to avoid committing secrets.

› vercel switchable freely interchangable memerylaiyrs


• Yes, but only if you design them as replaceable layers instead of binding the app directly to
  one vendor.

  Best shape:

  - UI/app layer talks to your own data interface
  - memory adapter layer implements that interface
  - provider backends sit underneath:
      - Supabase/Postgres
      - local files / source-chain
      - Holochain later
      - vector/embedding store later if needed

  - use Supabase only as one interchangeable backend
  - keep your app code calling something like MemoryStore, ClaimStore, ContextStore
  - never spread raw Supabase calls everywhere in the app

  Think in terms of ports/adapters:

  - MemoryStore.saveSession()
  - MemoryStore.loadContext()
  - ClaimStore.appendClaim()
  - DecisionStore.listRecent()

  Then implementations:

  - SupabaseMemoryStore
  - FileMemoryStore
  - HolochainMemoryStore
  - HybridMemoryStore

  That gives you “switchable freely interchangeable memory layers” for real, instead of a slogan.

  For FLOSSI0ULLK specifically, the plausible layering is:

  - L0: cheap session/bootstrap memory
  - L1: durable project memory / ADR / context summaries
  - L2: claims, votes, traces, source-chain artifacts
  - L3: future agent-centric runtime truth in Holochain

  If you want a Vercel-era bridge, do this:

  - Vercel app
  - Supabase as temporary durable store
  - adapter interfaces in code
  - export/import path so data can move out later
  - no vendor-specific logic in the domain layer

  So: Supabase is fine as a temporary memory substrate, but only if we treat it as a swap-in
  adapter, not canon.

  If you want, I can sketch the exact minimal architecture for a Vercel app with interchangeable
  memory layers in this repo.


› yes


• Use a separate web app inside the repo, with ports/adapters, and treat Supabase as a
  replaceable backend.

  Minimal shape

  FLOSS/
    apps/
      web/
        app/                 # Next.js or other Vercel app
        lib/
          domain/
            memory.ts
            claims.ts
            context.ts
          ports/
            MemoryStore.ts
            ClaimStore.ts
            ContextStore.ts
          adapters/
            supabase/
            file/
            holochain/
          server/
            memory-service.ts
            claim-service.ts

  Rule

  - UI never calls Supabase directly.
  - UI calls services.
  - Services depend on interfaces.
  - Adapters implement interfaces.

  Example ports:

  export interface MemoryStore {
    getSession(id: string): Promise<SessionMemory | null>
    putSession(input: SessionMemory): Promise<void>
    listRecent(limit: number): Promise<SessionMemory[]>
  }

  export interface ClaimStore {
    appendClaim(input: Claim): Promise<void>
    listClaims(scope: string): Promise<Claim[]>
  }

  Backends

  - SupabaseMemoryStore: first Vercel-era durable backend
  - FileMemoryStore: local/dev/export-import fallback
  - HolochainMemoryStore: later runtime-truth backend
  - optional HybridMemoryStore: write-through or read-fallback

  Single switch

  MEMORY_BACKEND=supabase

  Then server boot:

  const memoryStore =
    process.env.MEMORY_BACKEND === "supabase"
      ? new SupabaseMemoryStore(...)
      : process.env.MEMORY_BACKEND === "file"
      ? new FileMemoryStore(...)
      : new HolochainMemoryStore(...)

  Supabase tables
  Keep them generic:

  - memory_records
  - context_projections
  - claims
  - votes
  - decisions

  Core columns:

  - id
  - kind
  Minimum:
  - SUPABASE_URL
  - SUPABASE_SERVICE_ROLE_KEY

  Only if browser auth/client reads are needed:
  - NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_URL
  - NEXT_PUBLIC_SUPABASE_ANON_KEY
  - Session memory -> memory_records
  - L0/L1 context projections -> context_projections
  - claims/votes/decisions -> separate tables, not mixed into generic memory
  - later Holochain adapter can replace or shadow these stores

  Important boundary

  - Supabase is a temporary substrate
  - canon stays in repo/docs/source-chain
  - app data must support export/import JSONL so you can leave Supabase later

  Best repo move
  Create apps/web and leave current repo root/static surface alone. Don’t entangle the public
  landing page with the future memory app.

  If you want, I can scaffold this architecture in the repo next.
